<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41598"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The genome sequence of the outbreeding globe artichoke constructed de novo incorporating a phase-aware low-pass sequencing strategy of F 1 progeny"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Scientific Reports 2016 6:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2016-01-20"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2016 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Globe artichoke (Cynara cardunculus var. scolymus) is an out-crossing, perennial, multi-use crop species that is grown worldwide and belongs to the Compositae, one of the most successful Angiosperm families. We describe the first genome sequence of globe artichoke. The assembly, comprising of 13,588 scaffolds covering 725 of the 1,084&#8201;Mb genome, was generated using ~133-fold Illumina sequencing data and encodes 26,889 predicted genes. Re-sequencing (30&#215;) of globe artichoke and cultivated cardoon (C. cardunculus var. altilis) parental genotypes and low-coverage (0.5 to 1&#215;) genotyping-by-sequencing of 163 F1 individuals resulted in 73% of the assembled genome being anchored in 2,178 genetic bins ordered along 17 chromosomal pseudomolecules. This was achieved using a novel pipeline, SOILoCo (Scaffold Ordering by Imputation with Low Coverage), to detect heterozygous regions and assign parental haplotypes with low sequencing read depth and of unknown phase. SOILoCo provides a powerful tool for de novo genome analysis of outcrossing species. Our data will enable genome-scale analyses of evolutionary processes among crops, weeds and wild species within and beyond the Compositae and will facilitate the identification of economically important genes from related species."/>

We describe the first genome sequence of globe artichoke. SOILoCo provides a powerful tool for de novo genome analysis of outcrossing species. Our data will enable genome-scale analyses of evolutionary processes among crops, weeds and wild species within and beyond the Compositae and will facilitate the identification of economically important genes from related species."/>

0004 1936 9684, The Genome Center, University of California, Davis, CA, USA" /></span></sup><sup class="u-js-hide"> <a href="#na1">na1</a></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-author-list__item" href="#auth-Sergio-Lanteri" data-author-popup="auth-Sergio-Lanteri">Sergio Lanteri</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope"             <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Globe artichoke <i>(Cynara cardunculus</i> var. <i>scolymus)</i> is an out-crossing, perennial, multi-use crop species that is grown worldwide and belongs to the Compositae, one of the most successful Angiosperm families. We describe the first genome sequence of globe artichoke. The assembly, comprising of 13,588 scaffolds covering 725 of the 1,084 Mb genome, was generated using ~133-fold Illumina sequencing data and encodes 26,889 predicted genes. Re-sequencing (30×) of globe artichoke and cultivated cardoon (<i>C. cardunculus</i> var. <i>altilis</i>) parental genotypes and low-coverage (0.5 to 1×) genotyping-by-sequencing of 163 F<sub>1</sub> individuals resulted in 73% of the assembled genome being anchored in 2,178 genetic bins ordered along 17 chromosomal pseudomolecules. This was achieved using a novel pipeline, SOILoCo (Scaffold Ordering by Imputation with Low Coverage), to detect heterozygous regions and assign parental haplotypes with low sequencing read depth and of unknown phase. SOILoCo provides a powerful tool for <i>de novo</i> genome analysis of outcrossing species. Our data will enable genome-scale analyses of evolutionary processes among crops, weeds and wild species within and beyond the Compositae and will facilitate the identification of economically important genes from related species.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Globe artichoke (<i>Cynara cardunculus</i> var. <i>scolymus</i> L.) is an out-crossing perennial vegetable crop that is native to the Mediterranean Basin and grown worldwide. Its production has risen from about 1.5 Mt in 1993 to 1.8 Mt in 2013 (FAOSTAT, 2013) with a gross production value of about US$600 million. Italy, which harbours the richest primary gene pool of cultivated globe artichoke, is the top producing country, but globe artichoke cultivation has spread to other Mediterranean countries and in recent years to the Americas and China. In addition to globe artichoke, the <i>C. cardunculus</i> complex includes the cultivated cardoon (var. <i>altilis</i> DC) and the wild cardoon [var. <i>sylvestris</i> (Lamk) Fiori]. The cultivated forms have evolved independently from the wild cardoon in the Mediterranean Basin with selection for different plant characteristics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Portis, E., Barchi, L., Acquadro, A., Macua, J. &amp; Lanteri, S. Genetic diversity assessment in cultivated cardoon by AFLP (amplified fragment length polymorphism) and microsatellite markers. Plant Breed. 124, 299–304 (2005)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d46378e677">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Mauro, R. et al. Genetic diversity of globe artichoke landraces from Sicilian small-holdings: implications for evolution and domestication of the species. Cons. Genet. 10, 431–440 (2009)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d46378e680">2</a></sup>. The two cultivated and the wild forms are fully cross-compatible. The primary product of globe artichoke is the immature inflorescence (compound head or <i>capitulum</i>), whose inner bracts and fleshy receptacle are consumed as a fresh, preserved or frozen delicacy; as are other members of the <i>C. cardunculus</i> complex, it is also a source of bio-active compounds, such as antioxidant phenolics, sesquiterpene lactones and inulin<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Comino, C. et al. The isolation and mapping of a novel hydroxycinnamoyltransferase in the globe artichoke chlorogenic acid pathway. BMC Plant Biol. 9, 30 (2009)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d46378e690">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Lattanzio, V., Kroon, P., Linsalata, V. &amp; Cardinali, A. Globe artichoke: A functional food and source of nutraceutical ingredients. J. Funct. Foods 1, 131–144 (2009)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d46378e693">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Eljounaidi, K. et al. Cytochrome P450s from Cynara cardunculus L. CYP71AV9 and CYP71BL5, catalyze distinct hydroxylations in the sesquiterpene lactone biosynthetic pathway. Plant Sci. 223, 59–68 (2014)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d46378e696">5</a></sup> and can be exploited for the production of lignocellulosic biomass and seed oil for both edible and biofuel purposes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Ierna, A. &amp; Mauromicale, G. Cynara cardunculus L. genotypes as a crop for energy purposes in a Mediterranean environment. Biomass Bioenerg. 34, 754–760 (2010)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d46378e700">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Portis, E. et al. Potentiality of Cynara cardunculus L. as energy crop. J Biotechnol. 150, S165–S166 (2010)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d46378e703">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Foti, S. et al. Possible alternative utilization of Cynara spp. I. Biomass, grain yield and chemical composition of grain. Ind. Crop. Prod. 10, 219–228 (1999)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d46378e706">8</a></sup>.</p><p><i>C. cardunculus</i> is a diploid species (2n = 2× = 34) with a medium-sized genome estimated by flow cytometry to be 1.07 Gb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Marie, D. &amp; Brown, S. A cytometric exercise in plant DNA histograms, with 2C-values for 70 species. Biol. Cell 78, 41–51 (1993)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d46378e715">9</a></sup>. The few genomics data currently available for this crop include a restriction site associated DNA sequencing (RADseq) genome analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Scaglione, D. et al. RAD tag sequencing as a source of SNP markers in Cynara cardunculus L. BMC Genomics 13, 3 (2012)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d46378e719">10</a></sup> and RNAseq data, which provided a reference transcriptome of 38 K unigenes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Scaglione, D. et al. Large-scale transcriptome characterization and mass discovery of SNPs in globe artichoke and its related taxa. Plant Biotech. J. 10, 956–969 (2012)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d46378e723">11</a></sup>. In addition, several <i>C. cardunculus</i> genetic maps have been developed based on a two-way pseudo-testcross approach and QTL analyses for key breeding traits have been conducted<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Lanteri, S. et al. A first linkage map of globe artichoke (Cynara cardunculus var. scolymus L.) based on AFLP, S-SAP, M-AFLP and microsatellite markers. Theor. Appl. Genet. 112, 1532–1542 (2006)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d46378e730">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Portis, E. et al. Genetic mapping and identification of QTL for earliness in the globe artichoke/cultivated cardoon complex. BMC Res. Not. 5, 252 (2012)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d46378e733">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Portis, E. et al. Construction of a reference molecular linkage map of globe artichoke (Cynara cardunculus var. scolymus). Theor. Appl. Genet. 120, 59–70 (2009)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d46378e736">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Portis, E. et al. Mapping yield-associated QTL in globe artichoke. Mol. Breed. 34, 615–630 (2014)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d46378e739">15</a></sup>. The sequence of the chloroplast genome has recently been reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Curci, P., De Paola, D., Danzi, D., Vendramin, G. &amp; Sonnante, G. Complete Chloroplast Genome of the Multifunctional Crop Globe Artichoke and Comparison with Other Asteraceae. Plos One 10 (2015)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d46378e744">16</a></sup>.</p><p><i>C. cardunculus</i> is a member of the Compositae (a.k.a. Asteraceae), which is the largest and one of the most ecologically successful botanical families<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Magallon, S., Crane, P. &amp; Herendeen, P. Phylogenetic pattern, diversity and diversification of eudicots. Ann. Mo. Bot. Gard. 86, 297–372 (1999)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d46378e753">17</a></sup>. The family most likely originated in South America (30 to 100 Mya)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Raven, P. H. &amp; Axelrod, D. I. Angiosperm biogeography and past continental movements. Ann. Mo. Bot. Gard. 61, 39–637 (1974)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d46378e757">18</a></sup>. Rapid diversification of the Compositae in the mid-Eocene (40 Mya) is associated with a polyploidization event near the base of the family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Barker, M. et al. Multiple paleopolyploidizations during the evolution of the Compositae reveal parallel patterns of duplicate gene retention after millions of years. Mol. Biol. Evol. 25, 2445–2455 (2008)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d46378e761">19</a></sup> and it has been conjectured that the base chromosome number for the family is nine<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Kesseli, R. V. &amp; Michelmore, R. W. The Compositae: systematically fascinating but specifically neglected. In Genome Mapping in Plants, (eds Paterson, A. H. &amp; Landes, R. G. ) 179–191 (Co. Georgetown, TX, 1997)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d46378e765">20</a></sup>. Of the Compositae crop genomes, the 2.7 Gb lettuce genome (<a href="http://lgr.genomecenter.ucdavis.edu">http://lgr.genomecenter.ucdavis.edu</a>; GenomeProject ID: PRJNA68025) and the 3.5 Gb sunflower genome (<a href="http://www.sunflowergenome.org">http://www.sunflowergenome.org</a>; GenomeProject ID: PRJNA64989) have been released; however, neither genome has been formally published. The only published sequence of a Compositae species is that of the 335 Mb genome of horseweed (<i>Conyza canadensis</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Peng, Y. et al. De Novo Genome Assembly of the Economically Important Weed Horseweed Using Integrated Data from Multiple Sequencing Platforms. Plant Physiol. 166, 1241-+ (2014)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d46378e787">21</a></sup>. However, the sequence scaffolds of horseweed have not been placed into linkage groups or integrated into pseudomolecules, so its genome cannot be used for syntenic analyses. Beyond the Compositae, the genomes of several Asterid species (potato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Xu, X. et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189–194 (2011)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d46378e791">22</a></sup>; tomato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Sato, S. et al. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635–641 (2012)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d46378e795">23</a></sup>, pepper<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Kim, S. et al. Genome sequence of the hot pepper provides insights into the evolution of pungency in Capsicum species. Nat. Genet. 46, 270–278 (2014)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d46378e799">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Qin, C. et al. Whole-genome sequencing of cultivated and wild peppers provides insights into Capsicum domestication and specialization. P. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 111, 5135–5140 (2014)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d46378e802">25</a></sup> and eggplant<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Hirakawa, H. et al. Draft genome sequence of eggplant (Solanum melongena L.): the representative Solanum species indigenous to the old world. DNA Res. 1–12 (2014)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d46378e807">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Rotino, G. L. et al. An Eggplant (Solanum melongena L.) High Quality Genome Draft. In Plant and Animal Genome XXIInd - January 11–15, 2014 edn (San Diego (CA, USA), 2014)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d46378e810">27</a></sup>) have been recently sequenced; thus, the availability of the globe artichoke genome adds to our understanding of ancestral genome duplications<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Barker, M. et al. Multiple paleopolyploidizations during the evolution of the Compositae reveal parallel patterns of duplicate gene retention after millions of years. Mol. Biol. Evol. 25, 2445–2455 (2008)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d46378e814">19</a></sup> and contributes insights into the evolution of the Compositae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Kesseli, R. V. &amp; Michelmore, R. W. The Compositae: systematically fascinating but specifically neglected. In Genome Mapping in Plants, (eds Paterson, A. H. &amp; Landes, R. G. ) 179–191 (Co. Georgetown, TX, 1997)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d46378e818">20</a></sup>.</p><p>Understanding of the genome structure of globe artichoke provides the foundation for the molecular deciphering of complex traits, the establishment of gene orthology and the study of synteny across the family to strengthen community research. Short-read sequencing technologies offer a cost-effective opportunity for non-model species to gain access to the genomic era. However, chromosome-scale assembly of genomes remains an endeavor that is constrained by costs and limited analytical toolkits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Xie, W. et al. Parent-independent genotyping for constructing an ultrahigh-density linkage map based on population sequencing. P. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 107, 10578–10583 (2010)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d46378e825">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Mascher, M., Wu, S., St Amand, P., Stein, N. &amp; Poland, J. Application of genotyping-by-sequencing on semiconductor sequencing platforms: a comparison of genetic and reference-based marker ordering in barley. PLoS One 8, e76925 (2013)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d46378e828">29</a></sup>. We generated the reference genome for globe artichoke using a clone with low residual heterozygosity. To assign scaffolds to genetic bins ordered along chromosomal linkage groups, we implemented a novel pipeline named SOILoCo - Scaffold Ordering by Imputation with Low Coverage. This utilizes low coverage (&lt;1×) whole genome shotgun genotyping-by-sequencing of an F<sub>1</sub> progeny to reliably identify heterozygous loci as well as segregation data for the haplotypes derived from the heterozygous parental lines. Such haplotypes do not need to be present in the reference sequence. The globe artichoke sequence provided information on the overall genome organization and gene content, along with structural and functional annotation. We also inferred the timings of speciation, whole genome duplication and expansion of mobile elements.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>The Illumina Hiseq2000 platform was used for whole-genome shotgun sequencing of the globe artichoke clone designated ‘2C’. This clone had been obtained by three cycles of selfing (S<sub>3</sub>) and had a residual heterozygosity of approximately 10%, as assessed by analysis of 115 SSR markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Scaglione, D. et al. Ontology and diversity of transcript-associated microsatellites mined from a globe artichoke EST database. BMC Genomics 10, 454 (2009)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d46378e848">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Acquadro, A. et al. Genetic mapping and annotation of genomic microsatellites isolated from globe artichoke. Theor. Appl. Genet. 118, 1573–1587 (2009)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d46378e851">31</a></sup>. DNA was extracted from young leaves and one paired-end and two mate-pair libraries generated, consistent with requirements of the ALLPATHS-LG assembly software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Gnerre, S. et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. P. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 1513–1518 (2011)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d46378e855">32</a></sup>. The 100 + 100 bp reads of the paired-end library that had an average insert size 170 bp produced 67 Gb of raw data (equivalent to an estimated genome coverage of 62×), with a 96% efficiency in generating 3’-overlaps with an average overlap of 26 bp. The sequencing of the two mate-pair libraries, with average effective insert sizes of 2.5 Kb and 5.5 Kb, produced 56 Gb and 7 Gb of raw data respectively (corresponding to 52× and 6.5× genome coverages). After trimming of sequencing adapters and removal of low-quality bases, a total of 133.7 Gb were available for assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#Tab1">Table 1</a>). The analysis of K-mer spectra confirmed the limited residual heterozygosity of the 2C breeding line; this was markedly lower than the levels detected in the parental genotypes (globe artichoke and cultivated cardoon) of our mapping population (see below; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S1</a>). Based on K-mer statistics and sequencing depth<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Li, R. et al. The sequence and de novo assembly of the giant panda genome. Nature 463, 311–317 (2010)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d46378e866">33</a></sup>, the globe artichoke genome size was estimated to be 1,084 Mb, in close agreement with the previous estimation by flow cytofluorimetry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Marie, D. &amp; Brown, S. A cytometric exercise in plant DNA histograms, with 2C-values for 70 species. Biol. Cell 78, 41–51 (1993)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d46378e870">9</a></sup>. The error-correction routine implemented in ALLPATHS-LG resulted in 87.5% of the filtered paired-end reads being used in the assembly. The 50% of the <i>de novo</i> assembly (<i>N</i><sub>50</sub>) was included in 10,596 contigs of 17.5 Kb or larger, with 32% G + C content (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#Tab2">Table 2</a>). The contigs were assembled into 13,588 scaffolds (≥1,000 bp), with a <i>N</i><sub>50</sub> of 1,408 and a <i>L</i><sub>50</sub> equal to 125.9 Kb. The final assembly spanned approximately 724.7 Mb. The longest scaffold was 1.5 Mb and the median gap size within a scaffold was 660 bp. Scaffolds were finally filtered to remove chloroplast sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary information</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 <i>De novo</i> sequencing statistics.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/srep19427/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Genome assembly statistics.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/srep19427/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The assembly was compared to the available UniGene set<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Scaglione, D. et al. Large-scale transcriptome characterization and mass discovery of SNPs in globe artichoke and its related taxa. Plant Biotech. J. 10, 956–969 (2012)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d46378e1425">11</a></sup> to assess the coverage of the <i>C. cardunculus</i> gene space; 37,018 of the 38,726 unigenes (95.6%) aligned to the assembled genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Methods and Results</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S1a</a>). Unaligned ESTs were analysed by BLASTx as in<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Chamala, S. et al. Assembly and Validation of the Genome of the Nonmodel Basal Angiosperm Amborella. Science 342, 1516–1517 (2013)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d46378e1438">34</a></sup> against Viridiplantae proteins (Refseq); 675 (69%) of these had hits. These unigenes (1.7% of all available unigenes) can contribute to a hypothetical total of 27,564 gene models, which would reflects a predicted proportion of non-assembled genes up to 2.5%. The genomic draft assembly was also surveyed for the presence of 248 complete conserved eukaryotic genes (CEGs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genornes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061–1067 (2007)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d46378e1443">35</a></sup>) of which more than 85% were mapped (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S1b</a>). The inclusion of partial matches increased the percentage of mapped CEGs to 96%. While the yield of complete CEGs increased from less conserved (Group 1–80.3%) to highly conserved genes (Group 4–93.85%), the coverage obtained with partial alignments was similar among the groups (95.45% to 98.46%; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary information</a>). This suggests that most of the missing complete alignments were due to sequence divergence and thus, it can be concluded that at least 95% of the gene space was captured in our genome assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">A pipeline for anchoring scaffolds using low-coverage data</h3><p>Linkage analysis of an F<sub>1</sub> population of 163 individuals, obtained by crossing globe artichoke C3 as the female parent and cultivated cardoon Alt41 as the male parent, was performed to identify the genetic position of sequence scaffolds and to generate chromosomal pseudomolecules. To achieve this, we developed and implemented a novel method for genotyping-by-sequencing individuals of an F<sub>1</sub> progeny from low-pass sequencing data combined with conventional deep sequencing of the heterozygous parents, whose linkage phase was unknown. The method relies on the <i>de novo</i> identification of parental allelic phases (haplotype blocks), with no reliance on haplotypes being shared with the reference sequence assembly. The pipeline, whose workflow is depicted in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>, was named SOILoCo (Scaffold Ordering by Imputation with Low Coverage). It requires: (i) a draft assembly of a reference genome; (ii) whole-genome sequencing of each parent to a coverage of at least 20× (being permissive of haplotype reconstruction with HAPCUT; genomes with lower SNP frequencies may require larger fragments to obtain phase calls); and (iii) whole-genome sequencing of their segregating progenies to a coverage as low as 0.5× each. Illumina reads of parental lines were first mapped to the reference sequence assembly in order to identify the positions of robust heterozygous SNPs. The haplotype blocks of each parental genome sequence were subsequently built using the HAPCUT algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Bansal, V. &amp; Bafna, V. HapCUT: an efficient and accurate algorithm for the haplotype assembly problem. Bioinformatics 24, 1153–1159 (2008)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d46378e1471">36</a></sup>. SNP polymorphisms were then called with low-confidence for each progeny individual based on low sequence coverage. A set of custom Perl scripts was then applied in order to: (i) catalogue SNP sites expected to segregate in a test-cross fashion; (ii) split the two segregating haplotypes for each parent (i.e. segregating SNP sites from either one or the other homologous chromosomes, phase-0 and phase-1 in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>); (iii) recover SNP calls for each individual in any given haplotype block/segregating phase; (iv) invoke an Hidden Markov Model (HMM) engine to infer a haplotype block-wide genotype call (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary information</a>); and finally, (v) summarize data and generate a dataset of genotypes for each individual.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep19427/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep19427/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep19427_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep19427/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep19427_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="889" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Workflow schema for the SoiLoCo pipeline used to anchor the <i>C. cardunculus</i> scaffolds in chromosomal pseudomolecules.</p><p>Alignments of parental reads to the draft scaffolds were used to (i) identify potential heterozygous test-cross sites and (ii) to compute haplotype phases in both parents (P1 and P2). A multi-sample VCF file of all the progeny was then processed to identify informative heterozygous sites based on parental SNPs and the phase of haplotype blocks (TC-separator.pl, blue box). This assigned the sites according to which phase (i.e. homologous chromosomes) they are expected to segregate in. Subsequently, an HMM-based algorithm was used to impute the most likely genotypes of each haplotype block segregating in the progeny (gt-hmm.pl, red box). A LOD score was also calculated to permit filtering of ambiguous imputations. Genotype imputation from the two alternative segregating phases were then summarized; when there was a discordant call between phases, a majority rule was applied and the highest LOD score for each segregating haplotype used to impute the most likely genotype (TC-string-merger.pl, green box). After grouping markers, linkage maps were generated for each parent using reiterative ordering with the MSTmap software (<a href="http://alumni.cs.ucr.edu/~yonghui/mstmap.html">http://alumni.cs.ucr.edu/~yonghui/mstmap.html</a>) and error correction using a Perl implementation of SMOOTH algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="van Os, H., Stam, P., Visser, R. &amp; van Eck, H. SMOOTH: a statistical method for successful removal of genotyping errors from high-density genetic linkage data. Theor. Appl. Genet. 112, 187–194 (2005)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d46378e1505">37</a></sup>. Maps were finally merged to generate a consensus map and to maximize the resolution of the order and orientation of scaffolds in chromosomal pseudomolecules.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep19427/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Illumina sequencing of the two parental genotypes yielded a total of 520 M (52.0 Gb) and 467 M (46.8 Gb) raw reads, corresponding to a coverage of about 48× and 43× for C3 and Alt41, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S2</a>). After read trimming and mapping to the reference genome, 5,828,006 and 3,092,899 heterozygous SNP sites were called in the two parental genotypes. In the C3 genotype of globe artichoke, <i>de novo</i> haplotype blocks assembly allowed us to compute a total of 192,938 blocks, corresponding to 364 Mb (L<sub>50</sub>: 4,772 bp, N<sub>50</sub>: 19,921) and an average density of 1 SNP per 59 bp. In cultivated cardoon parent Alt41, 122,570 haplotype blocks were successfully identified, corresponding to 187 Mb (L<sub>50</sub>: 4104 bp, N<sub>50</sub>: 12,001) and an average density of 1 SNP per 59 bp. The identical SNP frequency of the parental haplotype blocks indicates that SNP-dense regions contributed most in extending haplotype blocks, while similar contiguities is consistent with our observation that the lower frequency of heterozygous SNP sites in Alt41 is due to long homozygous segments in the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>) rather than due to a genome-wide reduced level of polymorphism. After the extraction of candidate SNP sites segregating in a test cross fashion (TC-SNPs), 2,128,486 were retained in globe artichoke C3, which were scattered across 138,608 haplotype blocks; while in cultivated cardoon Alt41 1,356,998 were retained, which covered 78,458 haplotype blocks. By invoking the HMM engine and combining the complementary segregation information from either phase-0 or phase-1 polymorphic sites, we retrieved genotype calls for phased haplotype blocks with a minimum calling rate of 70%. Since this F<sub>1</sub> mapping population had previously been used for linkage mapping in which no extensive segregation distortion had been observed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Portis, E. et al. Construction of a reference molecular linkage map of globe artichoke (Cynara cardunculus var. scolymus). Theor. Appl. Genet. 120, 59–70 (2009)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d46378e1540">14</a></sup>, we filtered out blocks with Χ<sup>2</sup> test p-value &lt; 0.03. The pseudo-testcross segregation analysis for C3 generated genotype calls for 23,381 blocks, representing 4,234 unique scaffolds, while analysis of Alt41 provided 15,262 genotyped blocks for 2,779 scaffolds; 1,677 mapped scaffolds were shared between the C3 and Alt41 datasets. Using the two-way pseudo-testcross approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Portis, E. et al. Mapping yield-associated QTL in globe artichoke. Mol. Breed. 34, 615–630 (2014)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d46378e1546">15</a></sup>, an independent linkage analysis was carried out for each parent, defining 1,157 and 1,497 genetic bins for the globe artichoke C3 and the cultivated cardoon Alt41 maps, respectively. This anchored 445 Mb of the assembly as 23,366 markers in C3 and 358 Mb as 15,227 markers in Alt41. Correction of putative missed calls by a Perl implementation of the SMOOTH algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="van Os, H., Stam, P., Visser, R. &amp; van Eck, H. SMOOTH: a statistical method for successful removal of genotyping errors from high-density genetic linkage data. Theor. Appl. Genet. 112, 187–194 (2005)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d46378e1551">37</a></sup> and subsequent markers re-ordering was iterated twice and resulted in final maps spanning 1,460 cM in C3 and 1,748 cM in Alt41, with an average inter-marker distance of 1.28 cM and 1.18 cM, respectively. Maps were highly co-linear (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>) and the generation of a consensus map provided mutual disambiguation of positions within genetic bins. The final dataset comprised of 2,178 distinct genetic anchors ordered along 17 chromosomal pseudo-molecules (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S3</a>) that contained 5,322 scaffolds spanning 526 Mb. Of these, 936 scaffolds (210 Mb) were oriented by correlating the genetic position of each marker within a given scaffold along with their physical coordinates. Chromosomal pseudomolecules were numbered so as to be consistent with previously published linkage groups (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep19427/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep19427_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="690" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Characteristics of the globe artichoke genome.</p><p><b>(a)</b> Heat map of repeat density in the reference genome generated from globe artichoke 2C (blue, low; orange, high density); <b>(b)</b> Heat map of gene density in the reference genome; <b>(c)</b> density of heterozygous SNPs in the globe artichoke parental genotype, C3; <b>(d)</b> density of heterozygous SNPs in the cultivated cardoon parental genotype, Alt41; <b>(e)</b> extended homozygous regions in cultivated cardoon: the color of the boxes indicate whether they occur in repeat-rich regions (green) or gene-rich regions (red).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep19427/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep19427/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep19427/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep19427_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="439" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Anchoring the globe artichoke reference genome to the genetic maps and analysis robustness for each map.</p><p><b>(a)</b> Alignments of parental maps with the inferred consensus map in the middle for the first three <i>C. cardunculus</i> linkage groups. <b>(b)</b> Distribution of sizes (x axis) and genetic distances (y axis) for every possible pair of scaffolds separated by up to 5 cM when comparing the two parental maps. Pairs are shown as concordant (left) if their relative genetic position is maintained in both maps; discordant (right) if it is not. Scaffold size is reported as the smaller size of the two scaffolds in a pair (size and color of dots emphasize the scaffold size).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep19427/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In order to assess the robustness of our method for genetically anchoring <i>de novo</i> assembled scaffolds without the availability of a validated reference genome, we exploited the availability of the two maps generated for each parent. To do this, we made pairwise comparisons of scaffold doublets separated by 5 cM or less. Doublets were considered concordant when the two scaffolds maintained the same reciprocal position across the two maps. This analysis demonstrated that 23,558 pairs of scaffolds (87%) were collinear, while only 3,480 had discordant positions. Furthermore, when only pairs of scaffolds longer than 100 Kb were considered, the ratio of concordant pairs increased to 94% (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>). The discordant pairs may have been due to: (i) small differences in genome organization of the two taxa, or (ii) errors in the genotyping process caused by a lack of informative SNP sites or possibly by the presence of confounding false SNPs resulting from repetitive regions which were not filtered out from the alignment.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Gene space</h3><p>The annotation pipeline yielded 26,889 gene models (and a total of 27,121 predicted transcripts, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#Tab3">Table 3</a>), of which 23,895 were located in the chromosomal pseudomolecules and only 2,994 were unmapped (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#Tab4">Table 4</a>). Gene models covered a total of 146.5 Mb, of which 38 Mb were exons, with median length of 141 bp, containing 32.8 Mb of coding sequences with a median length of 127 bp; the remainder comprised of introns with median length of 334 bp and untranslated regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#Tab3">Table 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S2</a>). <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#Fig2">Figure 2</a> shows gene-rich regions along with repeat-dense regions and suggests the likely locations of the centromeric regions.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 Statistics on predicted genes.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/srep19427/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-4"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab4" data-test="table-caption">Table 4 Statistics on the predicted genes for each chromosomal pseudomolecule.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/srep19427/tables/4"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Out of the 27,121 predicted proteins, 19,554 (72%) were functionally annotated. Protein clustering resulted in 3,724 clusters with 21,850 sequences (80.5% of the total number of predicted proteins). Top 15 groups by size are presented in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S4</a>. To obtain an overview of the entire genome, protein annotations were counted across all the groups. The most common functionally annotated predicted proteins in globe artichoke had a protein kinase domain (primarily serine/threonine kinases and tyrosine kinases), followed by the Zinc finger domain and the Leucine Rich Repeats (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S5</a>).</p><p>Comparative analysis of globe artichoke proteins against <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, turnip (<i>Brassica rapa</i>), strawberry (<i>Fragaria vesca</i>), tomato (<i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>) and lettuce (<i>Lactuca sativa</i>) resulted in 25,981 clusters, which included 153,126 proteins out of 201,990 proteins across the six taxa. Of the 27,196 globe artichoke proteins, 20,509 were grouped into 14,810 gene families, of which 418 are unique families and 6,687 are singletons. A core set of 6,005 families was shared by the six species of which 2,911 were detected as single copy in all the species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S6A</a>). The largest number of gene clusters was shared between globe artichoke and tomato, presumably due to their closer evolutionary relationship (<i>vs</i>. <i>Arabidopsis</i>/strawberry, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). The shared gene clusters represent 79.4% (for globe artichoke) and 82.2% (for tomato) of their individual total gene clusters.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Figure 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep19427/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep19427/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep19427_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep19427/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep19427_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="402" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Venn diagram of orthologous gene clusters among Arabidopsis thaliana</p><p>(<b>a</b>), <i>Cynara cardunculus</i> (<b>c</b>), <i>Fragaria vesca</i> (<b>f</b>) and <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> (<b>s</b>), showing a total of 9,246 common gene clusters.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep19427/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We searched for gene families in which a particular species contained a higher number of genes than expected and 46 returned a significant deviation, mostly driven by expansions in either <i>Arabidopsis</i>, tomato, strawberry or lettuce. Four clusters, in which globe artichoke had an expanded number of genes, are reported in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S6B</a>. These are annotated as (i) Cystatin-like proteins with the cysteine proteases inhibitor signature (33 members), (ii) replication factor A, C-terminal domain (15 members), (iii) glycosyltransferase family 10 (fucosyltransferase, 8 members); none of these three groups of genes were physically clustered. A fourth group (27 members), containing genes encoding p450 class E enzyme group I proteins was physically clustered in chromosome 13 (15 out of 27 genes, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S3A</a>), in a region spanning 800 Kb interspersed with 44 other genes. The phylogenetic analysis suggested that the 15 genes resulted from serial duplication events (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S3B</a>).</p><p>Five other clusters were over-represented in both globe artichoke and lettuce (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S6B</a>). The first two encoded NB-LRR disease resistance related proteins (with 20 and 19 members), one of which was clustered on chromosome 10 in two separate regions of 2 Mb each (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S3A</a>). A third cluster encoded bulb-type mannose specific lectin associated protein kinases (19 members) that localized on three chromosomes (chr. 5, 5 members; chr. 6, 5 members and chr. 9, 4 members). A fourth group encoded pentatricopeptide proteins (36 members). The last group encoded an Allergen Related Protein family (13 members) that was clustered on chromosome 15 over a 1 Mb region. A total of 418 clusters (1,170 genes) were unique to globe artichoke, across the panel of six analyzed species. A GO enrichment analysis showed an over-representation in some genes, particularly in relation to “response to stress”, “phenylpropanoid biosynthesis” and “lipid biosynthesis” (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 3C</a>). The specific genes are widely distributed over the globe artichoke chromosomes; 18 regions had clusters of genes involved in the same biological process (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 6c</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S3C</a>). In particular, there are clusters for “Defense Response” on chromosomes 1 (39 loci), 10 (5 loci), 13 (19 loci) and 16 (4 loci), “Auxin-activated signaling pathway” on chromosomes 14 (3 loci), 15 (6 loci), 17 (4 loci) and “Regulation of transcription” on chromosomes 2 (6 loci) and 14 (3 loci).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Repetitive content of the genome</h3><p>Approximately 424 Mb (58.4%) of the assembly was repetitive (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a>), of which 41.73% could not be classified. The most abundant super-families were class <i>Ty/1 Copia</i> (27.84%) and <i>Gypsy</i> elements (16.48%) followed by class II DNA elements (7.66%). 14,449 complete LTR retrotransposon-like elements were predicted; of these, 13,080 clustered into 910 families, which were then reduced to 610 by means of 2,142 unambiguously clustered LTR elements. Subsequently, 354 clusters were successfully classified into families and sub-families, while 154 were assigned to main families (RLG - Retrotransposon LTR Gypsy, RLC - Retrotransposon LTR Copia and RIX - Retrotransposon Unclassified). The 12,107 unclustered elements were aligned with BLAST against TREP database (<a href="http://wheat.pw.usda.gov/ggpages/Repeats/">http://wheat.pw.usda.gov/ggpages/Repeats/</a>) resulting in 7,909 type/family assignments. Of all the annotated repetitive elements, 57% and 37% belonged to the <i>Gypsy</i> and <i>Copia</i> families, respectively. Insertion dates were calculated based on sequence divergence of the LTRs for all the clustered elements; an expansion was evident at 2.5 Mya (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>). Lower variability in the ages among the <i>Gypsy</i> elements was observed, with a tendency to be more stable across time, with the exception of the <i>Fatima</i> sub-family. Higher variability was detected between different subtypes of <i>Copia</i> elements, with certain sub-families showing a decrease in insertion events over time, while others had a higher proportion of young elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#Fig5">Fig. 5b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Figure 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep19427/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep19427/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep19427_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep19427/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep19427_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="874" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Repeat identification and dating in the genome of <i>C. cardunculus</i> and miRNA analysis.</p><p><b>(a)</b> Overall distribution of the repetitive fraction. <b>(b)</b> Distributions of insertion ages in different families of LTR elements. <b>(c)</b> Number of loci per miRNA considered as four frequency classes: (i) &lt;10 loci; (ii) 10 to 99 loci; (iii) 100 to 999 loci; (iv) &gt;1000 loci. <b>(d)</b> Distribution of Mixed, RR and NRR classes of miRNA within conserved miRNAs. <b>(e)</b> Distribution of Mixed, RR and NRR classes of miRNA within globe artichoke-specific miRNAs. RR = miRNAs represented exclusively by loci covered by repeat elements; NRR = miRNAs without any locus covered by repeat elements; Mixed = miRNAs which include both loci covered and not covered by repeats. Conserved miRNAs are present in at least two of the 11 tested species.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep19427/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Satellite and microsatellite sequences</h3><p>The inspection of the available assembly for the presence of satellite sequences allowed the identification of several monomers scattered across the genome. The most abundant where three monomers of 96 bp, 94 bp and 103 bp, found 493, 477 and 191 times, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S7</a>). Other satellite sequences have been identified with minor frequencies. The distribution of satellite motifs across the chromosomes showed a contextual enrichment within presumptive centromeres as suggested by density of repeats (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S8</a>). It was not possible to determine a single satellite motif involved in centromeres.</p><p>The 17 pseudomolecules and unmapped scaffolds were used for the bulk mining of SSR markers and for the construction of the first microsatellite marker database <i>CyMSatDB</i> (<i>Cynara cardunculus</i> MicroSatellite DataBase), available at <a href="http://www.artichokegenome.unito.it">http://www.artichokegenome.unito.it</a>. A total of about 177,200 perfect SSR motifs were identified, including about 27,280 compound SSRs. The SSR loci (namely <i>CyMSat</i>) identified were classified on the basis of the repeat motif and their distribution over the pseudomolecules (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S8</a>) as well as the number of repeat units<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Portis, E. et al. CyMSatDB: The Globe Artichoke (Cynara cardunculus var. scolymus) Microsatellite Database. In Plant and Animal Genome XXIIIrd - January 10–14, 2015 edn. (San Diego (CA, USA), 2015)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d46378e3029">38</a></sup>. Di-nucleotides are the most frequent (73.0%), followed by tri- (11.2%), tetra-nucleotides (6.1%) and mono- (4.7%); penta- and hexa-nucleotides are rare (2.4 and 2.5% respectively). Nearly 244,000 imperfect SSR motifs were identified.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">miRNA analysis</h3><p>Based on the sequence similarity to known miRNAs of 11 species with diverse evolutionary relationships to globe artichoke, 73 different miRNAs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S9</a>) were computationally predicted in the globe artichoke genome. Since their identification was driven by similarity to known miRNAs, the size of the globe artichoke miRNome was likely underestimated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Denoeud, F. et al. The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Science 345, 1181–1184 (2014)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d46378e3044">39</a></sup>, but sufficient to provide an overview of its basic genomic organization. Although some previously known, conserved miRNAs, such as miR162 and miR482, were not identified, possibly due to their loss or, more likely, to genomic loci missing in the assembly, candidate genes for all but one of the 19 conserved miRNAs reported in previous smallRNA–seq studies of leaves and roots of globe artichoke<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="De Paola, D., Cattonaro, F., Pignone, D. &amp; Sonnante, G. The miRNAome of globe artichoke: conserved and novel micro RNAs and target analysis. BMC Genomics 13, 41 (2012)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d46378e3048">40</a></sup> were detected. Fifty-eight miRNAs were represented by less than 10 loci, nine numbered between 10 and 99 loci and four between 100 and 999, while two had greater than 1,000 loci (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#Fig5">Fig. 5c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary file S2</a>). All the miRNAs with more than 100 loci were <i>C. cardunculus</i>-specific (referring to miRNAs present in mirBase release 21), although some low-copy species-specific miRNAs were also detected.</p><p>The 73 miRNAs of globe artichoke were grouped according to their relationship with repeated sequences in the genome (mainly SSRs, low complexity regions and TEs): (i) miRNAs represented exclusively by loci covered by repeat elements (RR-miRNAs), (ii) miRNAs without any locus covered by repeat elements (NRR-miRNAs) and (iii) miRNAs which included both loci covered and not-covered by repeats (Mixed-miRNAs) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S10</a>). Interestingly, the majority of miRNAs (~58%, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#Fig5">Fig. 5d</a>) present, besides <i>C. cardunculus</i>, in at least two of the 11 tested species belonged to the NRR group, while only ~21% of <i>C. cardunculus</i>-specific miRNAs felt in this group (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#Fig5">Fig. 5e</a>).</p><p>Analysis of putative miRNA-targets among the transcripts identified here was carried out on both conserved and putative non-conserved miRNAs using mature sequences present in miRBase, release 21 as a reference. In total, 297 target sites for 57 miRNAs were located in 256 transcripts (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary file S3a</a>). Both conserved and non-conserved microRNAs target multiple genes: as examples, miR396, miR156 and miR395 target 39, 26 and 23 genes, respectively and, among non-conserved miRNAs, miR6108, miR6111 and miR6114 target 28, 24 and 15 genes respectively. A GO enrichment analysis identified several categories that were enriched for miRNA-target transcripts (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary file S3b</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S3D</a>). For processes (P), enrichments were observed in: regulation of transcription (GO:0006355, GO:0045449), protein import/transport (GO:0017038, GO:0015031), protein localization in mitochondrion (GO:0070585), protein import/localization in nucleus (GO:0034504, GO:0006606). With respect to functions (F), enrichments were observed in: nucleic acid binding (GO:0003676), protein transporter activity (GO:0008565) and ATP binding (GO:0005524). For components (C) enrichments were present for: nucleus (GO:0005634) and mitochondrial membrane outer membrane translocase complex (GO:0005742).</p><p>Many conserved miRNAs were predicted to target known transcription factors related to plant development, morphology and flowering time. Examples include miR160 and ARF (Auxin Response Factor), miR156 and SQUAMOSA promoter binding-like proteins, miR164 and NAC-like proteins, miR172 and AP2-like proteins and miR171 and GRAS-like proteins. Putative artichoke-specific miRNAs mainly target genes associated with enzymes and physiological proteins involved in adaptation such as metal ion-binding proteins involved in ion transport in plasma membrane (miR6108), serine/threonine protein kinases (miR6111), metal ion binding proteins and ATP binding proteins (miR6114), nucleotide binding (miR6110), ATP binding proteins and Leucine Rich Repeat (LRR) as well as protein with serine/threonine kinase activity (miR6118).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Age of speciation</h3><p>Distributions of synonymous nucleotide substitutions (Ks) were analysed to investigate ages of speciation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep19427#Fig6">Fig. 6</a>). The shared whole genome duplication(s) at the base of the Compositae family were placed at Ks ~ 0.78 in <i>C. cardunculus</i> and Ks ~ 0.95 in <i>L. sativa</i>. However, by applying the Ks correction to compensate for nuclear rate heterogeneity across Compositae as estimated elsewhere<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Barker, M. et al. Multiple paleopolyploidizations during the evolution of the Compositae reveal parallel patterns of duplicate gene retention after millions of years. Mol. Biol. Evol. 25, 2445–2455 (2008)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d46378e3111">19</a></sup>, the <i>C. cardunculus</i> and <i>L. sativa</i> WGD were estimated at Ks = 0.84 and Ks = 0.87, respectively, thus providing further evidence for a common Compositae WGD. Divergence of <i>Cardueae</i> and <i>Cichorieae</i> was calculated at Ks ~ 0.55 and compared with that obtained for pepper and tomato (Ks = 0.27) with the same analysis pipeline, consistent with the previous similar estimation (Ks = 0.30)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Kim, S. et al. Genome sequence of the hot pepper provides insights into the evolution of pungency in Capsicum species. Nat. Genet. 46, 270–278 (2014)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d46378e3128">24</a></sup>. Assuming the divergence of pepper and tomato at 19.1 Mya, this places the time of divergence of the <i>Cardueae</i>, <i>Cichorieae</i> and <i>Heliatheae</i> at 35 to 40 Mya.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Figure 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep19427/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep19427/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep19427_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep19427/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep19427_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="353" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Distribution of synonymous substitutions across paralogs and orthologs as defined through conservation of syntenic blocks.</p><p>Lettuce and globe artichoke speciation is described with a Ks peak of ~0.55, while ancestral common genome duplication predating the Euasterids II radiation is described by a Ks ~ 0.78 for globe artichoke and ~0.87 for lettuce prior to heterogeneity rate correction, confirming that no WGD occurred after speciation of <i>Chicoriae</i> and <i>Cardueae</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep19427/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>Here we report the first published genome sequence of a Compositae crop species and make these new genomic resources fully available to the scientific community. The Compositae is the largest family of flowering plants and its ecological success has served as a stimulus for studies of the mechanisms driving speciation and adaptation in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Barker, M. et al. Multiple paleopolyploidizations during the evolution of the Compositae reveal parallel patterns of duplicate gene retention after millions of years. Mol. Biol. Evol. 25, 2445–2455 (2008)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d46378e3177">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Barres, L. et al. Reconstructing the evolution and biogeographic history of tribe Cardueae (Compositae). American Journal of Botany 100, 867–882 (2013)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d46378e3180">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Crepet, W. &amp; Niklas, K. Darwin’s second “abominable mystery”: why are there so many angiosperm species? American Journal of Botany 96, 366–381 (2009)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d46378e3183">42</a></sup>. Our data will enable genome-scale analyses of evolutionary processes among crops, weeds and wild species both within and beyond the Compositae and will facilitate research and crop improvement in this large and economically important family.</p><p>The construction of a reference genome for a highly heterozygous, outbreeding species like globe artichoke is more challenging than for homozygous, inbreeding species. The analysis of a nearly homozygous genotype can facilitate the assembly of a single representative sequence with de Brujin graph-based algorithms. High density genetic analysis is an efficient strategy to validate assemblies and assign contigs and scaffolds to genetic bins ordered along chromosomal linkage groups. Next generation sequencing offers several methods to collect massive amounts of genotyping data in a cost-effective manner. Some rely on complexity reduction with restriction enzymes (GBS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Elshire, R. et al. A robust, simple genotyping-by-sequencing (GBS) approach for high diversity species. PLoS One 6, e19379 (2011)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d46378e3190">43</a></sup>, RADseq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Etter, P., Preston, J., Bassham, S., Cresko, W. &amp; Johnson, E. Local de novo assembly of RAD paired-end contigs using short sequencing reads. PLoS One 6, e18561 (2011)." href="/articles/srep19427#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d46378e3194">44</a></sup>, ddRAD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Peterson, B., Weber, J., Kay, E., Fisher, H. &amp; Hoekstra, H. Double Digest RADseq: an inexpensive method for de novo SNP discovery and genotyping in model and non-model species.
